THE CASE FOR THE PROSECUTION CHAPTER XII. THE LAST LINK CHAPTER XIII. POIROT EXPLAINS CHAPTER I. I GO TO STYLES The intense interest aroused in the public by what was known at the time as
``The Styles Case'' (the public)
has now somewhat subsided. Nevertheless, in view of the world-wide notoriety which attended it, I have been asked, both by my friend Poirot and the family themselves, to write an account of the whole story. This, we trust, will effectually silence the sensational rumours which still persist. I will therefore briefly set down the circumstances which led to my being connected with the affair. I had been invalided home from the Front; and, after spending some months in a rather depressing Convalescent Home, was given a month's sick leave. Having no near relations or friends, I was trying to make up my mind what to do, when I ran across John Cavendish. I had seen very little of him (Cavendish) for some years. Indeed, I had never known him (Cavendish) particularly well. He (Cavendish) was a good fifteen years my senior, for one thing, though he (Cavendish) hardly looked his (Cavendish) forty-five years. As a boy, though, I had often stayed at Styles, his (Cavendish) mother's place in Essex.

We had a good yarn about old times, and it ended in his (Cavendish) inviting me down to Styles to spend my leave there.

``The mater will be delighted to see you again--after all those years,'' (he)
he (Cavendish) added.
``Your mother keeps well? '' (I)
I asked. '' Oh, yes. I suppose you know that she (mother) has married again? '' I am afraid I showed my surprise rather plainly. Mrs. Cavendish, who had married John's father when he (John) was a widower with two sons, had been a handsome woman of middle-age as I remembered her (woman). She (woman) certainly could not be a day less than seventy now. I recalled her (woman) as an energetic, autocratic personality, somewhat inclined to charitable and social notoriety, with a fondness for opening bazaars and playing the Lady Bountiful. She (Lady) was a most generous woman, and possessed a considerable fortune of her (Lady) own. Their country-place, Styles Court, had been purchased by Mr. Cavendish early in their married life. He (Cavendish) had been completely under his (Cavendish) wife's ascendancy, so much so that, on dying, he (Cavendish) left the place to her (wife) for her (wife) lifetime, as well as the larger part of his (Cavendish) income; an arrangement that was distinctly unfair to his (Cavendish) two sons. Their step-mother, however, had always been most generous to them; indeed, they were so young at the time of their father's remarriage that they always thought of her (wife) as their own mother. Lawrence, the younger, had been a delicate youth. He (Lawrence) had qualified as a doctor but early relinquished the profession of medicine, and lived at home while pursuing literary ambitions; though his (Lawrence) verses never had any marked success. John practiced for some time as a barrister, but had finally settled down to the more congenial life of a country squire.

He (squire) had married two years ago, and had taken his (squire) wife to live at Styles, though I entertained a shrewd suspicion that he (squire) would have preferred his (squire) mother to increase his (squire) allowance, which would have enabled him (squire) to have a home of his (squire) own. Mrs. Cavendish, however, was a lady who liked to make her (Cavendish) own plans, and expected other people to fall in with them, and in this case she (Cavendish) certainly had the whip hand, namely: the purse strings. John noticed my surprise at the news of his (John) mother's remarriage and smiled rather ruefully. '' Rotten little bounder too!'' he (John) said savagely.

``I can tell you, Hastings, it's making life jolly difficult for us. As for Evie--you remember Evie?'' (unknown)
``No.'' (unknown)
``Oh, I suppose she (Evie) was after your time. She (Evie)'s the mater's factotum, companion, Jack of all trades! A great sport--old Evie! Not precisely young and beautiful, but as game as they make them.'' (unknown)
``You were going to say--?'' (unknown)
``Oh, this fellow! He (fellow) turned up from nowhere, on the pretext of being a second cousin or something of Evie's, though she (Evie) didn't seem particularly keen to acknowledge the relationship. The fellow is an absolute outsider, anyone can see that. He (outsider)'s got a great black beard, and wears patent leather boots in all weathers! But the mater cottoned to him (outsider) at once, took him (outsider) on as secretary--you know how she (Evie)'s always running a hundred societies? '' (I)
I nodded. '' Well, of course the war has turned the hundreds into thousands. No doubt the fellow was very useful to her (Evie). But you could have knocked us all down with a feather when, three months ago, she (Evie) suddenly announced that she (Evie) and Alfred were engaged! The fellow must be at least twenty years younger than she (Evie) is!

It's simply bare-faced fortune hunting; but there you are-- she (Evie) is her (Evie) own mistress, and she (Evie)'s married him (fellow).''

``It must be a difficult situation for you all.'' (unknown)
``Difficult! It's damnable!'' (I)
Thus it came about that, three days later, I descended from the train at Styles St. Mary, an absurd little station, with no apparent reason for existence, perched up in the midst of green fields and country lanes. John Cavendish was waiting on the platform, and piloted me out to the car. '' Got a drop or two of petrol still, you see,'' he (Cavendish) remarked. '' Mainly owing to the mater's activities. '' The village of Styles St. Mary was situated about two miles from the little station, and Styles Court lay a mile the other side of it. It was a still, warm day in early July. As one looked out over the flat Essex country, lying so green and peaceful under the afternoon sun, it seemed almost impossible to believe that, not so very far away, a great war was running its appointed course. I felt I had suddenly strayed into another world. As we turned in at the lodge gates, John said:
``I'm afraid you'll find it very quiet down here, Hastings.'' (John/John Cavendish/Mr. Cavendish)
``My dear fellow, that's just what I want.'' (unknown)
``Oh, it's pleasant enough if you want to lead the idle life. I drill with the volunteers twice a week, and lend a hand at the farms. My wife works regularly `on the land'. She (wife) is up at five every morning to milk, and keeps at it steadily until lunchtime. It's a jolly good life taking it all round--if it weren't for that fellow Alfred Inglethorp!'' (He)
He (Inglethorp) checked the car suddenly, and glanced at his (Inglethorp) watch.

Her (Howard) conversation, I soon found, was couched in the telegraphic style.

I shall never forget them. She (woman) greeted me with a few words of pleasant welcome in a low clear voice, and I sank into a basket chair feeling distinctly glad that I had accepted John's invitation. An appreciative listener is always stimulating, and I described, in a humorous manner, certain incidents of my Convalescent Home, in a way which, I flatter myself, greatly amused my hostess. John, of course, good fellow though he (fellow) is, could hardly be called a brilliant conversationalist. At that moment a well remembered voice floated through the open French window near at hand:

``Then you'll write to the Princess after tea, Alfred? I'll write to Lady Tadminster for the second day, myself. Or shall we wait until we hear from the Princess? In case of a refusal, Lady Tadminster might open it the first day, and Mrs. Crosbie the second. Then there's the Duchess--about the school fete.

'' (a man)

Just for a moment I had a premonition of approaching evil. CHAPTER II. THE 16TH AND 17TH OF JULY I had arrived at Styles on the 5th of July. I come now to the events of the 16th and 17th of that month. For the convenience of the reader I will recapitulate the incidents of those days in as exact a manner as possible. They were elicited subsequently at the trial by a process of long and tedious cross-examinations. I received a letter from Evelyn Howard a couple of days after her (Howard) departure, telling me she (Howard) was working as a nurse at the big hospital in Middlingham, a manufacturing town some fifteen miles away, and begging me to let her (Howard) know if Mrs. Inglethorp should show any wish to be reconciled. CHAPTER III. THE NIGHT OF THE TRAGEDY To make this part of my story clear, I append the following plan of the first floor of Styles. The servants' rooms are reached through the door B. They have no communication with the right wing, where the Inglethorps' rooms were situated. It seemed to be the middle of the night when I was awakened by Lawrence Cavendish. He (Cavendish) had a candle in his (Cavendish) hand, and the agitation of his (Cavendish) face told me at once that something was seriously wrong.
``What's the matter? '' (I)

``We are afraid my mother is very ill. She (mother) seems to be having some kind of fit. Unfortunately she (mother) has locked herself in.'' (unknown)
``I'll come at once. '' (I)
I sprang out of bed; and, pulling on a dressing-gown, followed Lawrence along the passage and the gallery to the right wing of the house. John Cavendish joined us, and one or two of the servants were standing round in a state of awe-stricken excitement. Lawrence turned to his (Lawrence) brother.

``What do you think we had better do?'' (I)
Never, I thought, had his (Lawrence) indecision of character been more apparent. John rattled the handle of Mrs. Inglethorp's door violently, but with no effect. It was obviously locked or bolted on the inside. The whole household was aroused by now. The most alarming sounds were audible from the interior of the room. Clearly something must be done. '' Try going through Mr. Inglethorp's room, sir,'' cried Dorcas. '' Oh, the poor mistress!'' Suddenly I realized that Alfred Inglethorp was not with us--that he (Inglethorp) alone had given no sign of his (Inglethorp) presence. John opened the door of his (John) room. We went straight to the connecting door. That, too, was locked or bolted on the inside. What was to be done? '' Oh, dear, sir,'' cried Dorcas, wringing her (Dorcas) hands,
``what ever shall we do?'' (Dorcas/Mademoiselle Dorcas)
``We must try and break the door in, I suppose. It'll be a tough job, though. Here, let one of the maids go down and wake Baily and tell him (Dorcas) to go for Dr. Wilkins at once. Now then, we'll have a try at the door. Half a moment, though, isn't there a door into Miss Cynthia's rooms?'' (unknown)
``Yes, sir, but that's always bolted. It's never been undone.'' (unknown)
``Well, we might just see.'' (He)

I think this one is a shade less solid than the one in the passage. '' We strained and heaved together. The framework of the door was solid, and for a long time it resisted our efforts, but at last we felt it give beneath our weight, and finally, with a resounding crash, it was burst open. POIROT INVESTIGATES The house which the Belgians occupied in the village was quite close to the park gates. The papers, of course, had been full of the tragedy. Glaring headlines, sandwiched biographies of every member of the household, subtle innuendoes, the usual familiar tag about the police having a clue. Nothing was spared us. It was a slack time. The war was momentarily inactive, and the newspapers seized with avidity on this crime in fashionable life: '' The Mysterious Affair at Styles'' was the topic of the moment. Naturally it was very annoying for the Cavendishes.

The house was constantly besieged by reporters, who were consistently denied admission, but who continued to haunt the village and the grounds, where they lay in wait with cameras, for any unwary members of the household. We all lived in a blast of publicity. The Scotland Yard men came and went, examining, questioning, lynx-eyed and reserved of tongue. Towards what end they were working, we did not know. Had they any clue, or would the whole thing remain in the category of undiscovered crimes? I HAD had no opportunity as yet of passing on Poirot's message to Lawrence. Sir Ernest Heavywether, the famous K. C., had been engaged to defend him (Heavywether). Mr. Philips, K. C., opened the case for the Crown. The murder, he (Philips) said, was a most premeditated and cold-blooded one. It was neither more nor less than the deliberate poisoning of a fond and trusting woman by the stepson to whom she (woman) had been more than a mother. Ever since his (stepson) boyhood, she (woman) had supported him (stepson). He (stepson) and his (stepson) wife had lived at Styles Court in every luxury, surrounded by her (woman) care and attention. She (woman) had been their kind and generous benefactress. He (stepson) proposed to call witnesses to show how the prisoner, a profligate and spendthrift, had been at the end of his (prisoner) financial tether, and had also been carrying on an intrigue with a certain Mrs. Raikes, a neighbouring farmer's wife.

This having come to his (farmer) stepmother's ears, she (stepmother) taxed him (farmer) with it on the afternoon before her (stepmother) death, and a quarrel ensued, part of which was overheard. On the previous day, the prisoner had purchased strychnine at the village chemist's shop, wearing a disguise by means of which he (prisoner) hoped to throw the onus of the crime upon another man--to wit, Mrs. Inglethorp's husband, of whom he (husband) had been bitterly jealous. Luckily for Mr. Inglethorp, he (Inglethorp) had been able to produce an unimpeachable alibi. On the afternoon of July 17th, continued Counsel, immediately after the quarrel with her (Inglethorp) son, Mrs. Inglethorp made a new will. This will was found destroyed in the grate of her (Inglethorp) bedroom the following morning, but evidence had come to light which showed that it had been drawn up in favour of her (Inglethorp) husband. Deceased had already made a will in his (husband) favour before her (Inglethorp) marriage, but--and Mr. Philips wagged an expressive forefinger--the prisoner was not aware of that. What had induced the deceased to make a fresh will, with the old one still extant, he (prisoner) could not say. She (Inglethorp) was an old lady, and might possibly have forgotten the former one; or--this seemed to him (prisoner) more likely --she (Inglethorp) may have had an idea that it was revoked by her (Inglethorp) marriage, as there had been some conversation on the subject. Ladies were not always very well versed in legal knowledge. She (Inglethorp) had, about a year before, executed a will in favour of the prisoner. He (prisoner) would call evidence to show that it was the prisoner who ultimately handed his (prisoner) stepmother her (stepmother) coffee on the fatal night.

Later in the evening, he (prisoner) had sought admission to her (stepmother) room, on which occasion, no doubt, he (prisoner) found an opportunity of destroying the will which, as far as he (prisoner) knew, would render the one in his (prisoner) favour valid. It would be for the jury to decide whether or not these damning facts constituted an overwhelming proof of the prisoner's guilt. And, subtly implying that a jury which did not so decide, was quite unthinkable, Mr. Philips sat down and wiped his (Philips) forehead. To begin with, the fragment corresponds exactly with a tear in Mrs. Cavendish's armlet. I gave a gasp. What idiots we had been never to think of that fire as being incongruous! Poirot was continuing:
``The temperature on that day, messieurs, was 80 degrees in the shade. Yet Mrs. Inglethorp ordered a fire! Why? Because she (Inglethorp) wished to destroy something, and could think of no other way. You will remember that, in consequence of the War economics practiced at Styles, no waste paper was thrown away. There was therefore no means of destroying a thick document such as a will. The moment I heard of a fire being lighted in Mrs. Inglethorp's room, I leaped to the conclusion that it was to destroy some important document--possibly a will. So the discovery of the charred fragment in the grate was no surprise to me. I did not, of course, know at the time that the will in question had only been made this afternoon, and I will admit that, when I learnt that fact, I fell into a grievous error. I came to the conclusion that Mrs. Inglethorp's determination to destroy her (Inglethorp) will arose as a direct consequence of the quarrel she (Inglethorp) had that afternoon, and that therefore the quarrel took place after, and not before the making of the will. '' (Poirot/POIROT/Hercule Poirot)

'' I conjectured, and conjectured rightly, that these words were addressed, not to her (wife) husband, but to Mr. John Cavendish. She (wife) admits to Dorcas, 'I don't know what to do; scandal between husband and wife is a dreadful thing.' At 4 o'clock she (wife) has been angry, but completely mistress of herself. At 5 o'clock she (wife) is in violent distress, and speaks of having had a great shock. '' Looking at the matter psychologically, I drew one deduction which I was convinced was correct. The second `scandal' she (wife) spoke of was not the same as the first--and it concerned herself! '' Let us reconstruct. At 4 o'clock, Mrs. Inglethorp quarrels with her (Inglethorp) son, and threatens to denounce him (son) to his (son) wife--who, by the way, overheard the greater part of the conversation. Presumably, then, between 4.30 and 5 o'clock, something has occurred to occasion a complete revolution of feeling, since she (Dorcas) is now as anxious to destroy the will, as she (Dorcas) was before to make it. What was that something? '' As far as we know, she (Dorcas) was quite alone during that half-hour. Nobody entered or left that boudoir. What then occasioned this sudden change of sentiment?

``One can only guess, but I believe my guess to be correct. Mrs. Inglethorp had no stamps in her (Inglethorp) desk.

We know this, because later she (Inglethorp) asked Dorcas to bring her (Dorcas) some. Now in the opposite corner of the room stood her (Dorcas) husband's desk--locked. She (Dorcas) was anxious to find some stamps, and, according to my theory, she (Dorcas) tried her (Dorcas) own keys in the desk. That one of them fitted I know. She (Dorcas) therefore opened the desk, and in searching for the stamps she (Dorcas) came across something else--that slip of paper which Dorcas saw in her (Dorcas) hand, and which assuredly was never meant for Mrs. Inglethorp's eyes. She (Inglethorp) knew that her (Inglethorp) mother-in-law invariably kept all important papers in this particular case. '' (Mrs. Cavendish)

Then I discovered that I had been guilty of a very grave oversight. Coffee had been brought in for seven persons, not six, for Dr. Bauerstein had been there that evening. This changed the face of the whole affair, for there was now one cup missing. ``I was confident that the missing cup was that of Mademoiselle Cynthia. ``We are now able to account for the symptoms of strychnine poisoning being so long in making their appearance. A narcotic taken with strychnine will delay the action of the poison for some hours.'' (Poirot/POIROT/Hercule Poirot)
Poirot paused. Mary looked up at him (Poirot), the colour slowly rising in her (Mary) face.

``What? '' (unknown)

``Do you mean that the murderer introduced the strychnine into her (Inglethorp) tonic? '' (I)

To make that clear to you, I will read you an extract from a book on dispensing which I found in the Dispensary of the Red Cross Hospital at Tadminster: '' (unknown)
`The following prescription has become famous in text books: Strychninae Sulph... . gr. I Potass Bromide ... 3vi Aqua ad... 3viii Fiat Mistura This solution deposits in a few hours the greater part of the strychnine salt as an insoluble bromide in transparent crystals. A lady in England lost her (lady) life by taking a similar mixture: the precipitated strychnine collected at the bottom, and in taking the last dose she (lady) swallowed nearly all of it!''

``Now there was, of course, no bromide in Dr. Wilkins' prescription, but you will remember that I mentioned an empty box of bromide powders. One or two of those powders introduced into the full bottle of medicine would effectually precipitate the strychnine, as the book describes, and cause it to be taken in the last dose. You will learn later that the person who usually poured out Mrs. Inglethorp's medicine was always extremely careful not to shake the bottle, but to leave the sediment at the bottom of it undisturbed.

``Throughout the case, there have been evidences that the tragedy was intended to take place on Monday evening. (he)

A false step--' '' Here, my friends, the letter breaks off. Doubtless the writer was interrupted; but there can be no question as to his (writer) identity. We all know this hand-writing and--'' A howl that was almost a scream broke the silence. '' You devil! How did you get it? '' A chair was overturned. Poirot skipped nimbly aside. ``let me introduce you to the murderer, Mr. Alfred Inglethorp!'' (Poirot/POIROT/Hercule Poirot)

'' (John/John Cavendish/Mr. Cavendish)

``But why? It was most obvious. Did not Monsieur Lawrence make the sour face every time Mademoiselle Cynthia spoke and laughed with his (Lawrence) brother? He (Lawrence) had taken it into his (Lawrence) long head that Mademoiselle Cynthia was in love with Monsieur John. When he (John) entered his (John) mother's room, and saw her (Cynthia) obviously poisoned, he (John) jumped to the conclusion that Mademoiselle Cynthia knew something about the matter. He (John) was nearly driven desperate. First he (John) crushed the coffee-cup to powder under his (John) feet, remembering that she (Cynthia) had gone up with his (John) mother the night before, and he (John) determined that there should be no chance of testing its contents. Thenceforward, he (John) strenuously, and quite uselessly, upheld the theory of `Death from natural causes'.'' (unknown)
``And what about the `extra coffee-cup'?'' (unknown)
``I was fairly certain that it was Mrs. Cavendish who had hidden it, but I had to make sure. Monsieur Lawrence did not know at all what I meant; but, on reflection, he (Lawrence) came to the conclusion that if he (Lawrence) could find an extra coffee-cup anywhere his (Lawrence) lady love would be cleared of suspicion. And he (Lawrence) was perfectly right.'' (unknown)
``One thing more. What did Mrs. Inglethorp mean by her (Inglethorp) dying words?'' (unknown)
``They were, of course, an accusation against her (Inglethorp) husband.'' (unknown)
``Dear me, Poirot,'' (I)
I said with a sigh,
``I think you have explained everything. I am glad it has all ended so happily. Even John and his (John) wife are reconciled.'' (I)
``Thanks to me.'' (unknown)
``How do you mean--thanks to you?'' (unknown)
``My dear friend, do you not realize that it was simply and solely the trial which has brought them together again?

That John Cavendish still loved his (Cavendish) wife , I was convinced. Also, that she (wife) was equally in love with him (Cavendish). But they had drifted very far apart. It all arose from a misunderstanding. She (wife) married him (Cavendish) without love. He (Cavendish) knew it. He (Cavendish) is a sensitive man in his (Cavendish) way, he (Cavendish) would not force himself upon her (wife) if she (wife) did not want him (Cavendish). And, as he (Cavendish) withdrew, her (wife) love awoke. But they are both unusually proud, and their pride held them inexorably apart. He (Cavendish) drifted into an entanglement with Mrs. Raikes, and she (Raikes) deliberately cultivated the friendship of Dr. Bauerstein. Do you remember the day of John Cavendish's arrest, when you found me deliberating over a big decision?'' (unknown)

``Yes, I quite understood your distress.'' (unknown)
``Pardon me, mon ami , but you did not understand it in the least. I was trying to decide whether or not I would clear John Cavendish at once. I could have cleared him (Cavendish)--though it might have meant a failure to convict the real criminals. They were entirely in the dark as to my real attitude up to the very last moment--which partly accounts for my success.'' (unknown)
``Do you mean that you could have saved John Cavendish from being brought to trial?'' (unknown)
``Yes, my friend. But I eventually decided in favour of 'a woman's happiness'. Nothing but the great danger through which they have passed could have brought these two proud souls together again. '' (I)
I looked at Poirot in silent amazement. The colossal cheek of the little man! Who on earth but Poirot would have thought of a trial for murder as a restorer of conjugal happiness!
``I perceive your thoughts, mon ami ,'' (Poirot/POIROT/Hercule Poirot)
said Poirot, smiling at me. '' No one but Hercule Poirot would have attempted such a thing!

And you are wrong in condemning it. The happiness of one man and one woman is the greatest thing in all the world.'' His (man) words took me back to earlier events. I remembered Mary as she (Mary) lay white and exhausted on the sofa, listening, listening. There had come the sound of the bell below. She (Mary) had started up. Poirot had opened the door, and meeting her (Mary) agonized eyes had nodded gently. '' Yes, madame,'' he (Poirot) said.

``I have brought him (Poirot) back to you.'' (He)
He (Poirot) had stood aside, and as I went out I had seen the look in Mary's eyes, as John Cavendish had caught his (Cavendish) wife in his (Cavendish) arms. '' Perhaps you are right, Poirot,'' I said gently.
``Yes, it is the greatest thing in the world.'' (Cynthia/Mademoiselle Cynthia/Mademoiselle)
Suddenly, there was a tap at the door, and Cynthia peeped in.
``I--I only--'' (I)
``Come in,'' (I)
I said, springing up. She (Cynthia) came in, but did not sit down.
``I--only wanted to tell you something--'' (Cynthia/Mademoiselle Cynthia/Mademoiselle)
``Yes?'' (Cynthia/Mademoiselle Cynthia/Mademoiselle)
Cynthia fidgeted with a little tassel for some moments, then, suddenly exclaiming:
``You dears!'' (Cynthia/Mademoiselle Cynthia/Mademoiselle)
kissed first me and then Poirot, and rushed out of the room again.
``What on earth does this mean? '' (I)
I asked, surprised. It was very nice to be kissed by Cynthia, but the publicity of the salute rather impaired the pleasure.
``It means that she (Cynthia) has discovered Monsieur Lawrence does not dislike her (Cynthia) as much as she (Cynthia) thought,'' (Poirot/POIROT/Hercule Poirot)
replied Poirot philosophically. '' But--''
``Here he (Poirot) is. '' (Lawrence/Monsieur Lawrence)
Lawrence at that moment passed the door. '' Eh! Monsieur Lawrence,'' called Poirot.
``We must congratulate you, is it not so?'' (Lawrence/Monsieur Lawrence)
Lawrence blushed, and then smiled awkwardly. A man in love is a sorry spectacle. Now Cynthia had looked charming. I sighed.
``What is it, mon ami? '' (unknown)
``Nothing,'' (I)
I said sadly.
